      .
      .

The columns are interpreted like so:

  1. The time, in seconds.  This actually doesn't come from yaLVDC; rather, it relates to the time at which the telemetry was received by analyzeBoost.py, measured from the time analyzeBoost.py itself started up.  So if you were to pause the emulated CPU, or if the emulated CPU is itself running faster or slower than real time due to your selected command-line switches, the timestamp might not be terribly meaningful from the perspective of what's going on within the CPU.
  2. The name of the flight-program variable which is being telemetered.
  3. The raw data value, either as an signed decimal number or (if prefixed by a capital-O) an unsigned octal.
  4. The binary scaling applicable to the raw data.  If two different scalings are listed, it's because the data is scaled differently in the boost phase than it is when the vehicle is in its target orbit.  If no scaling factor is listed at all, that's because no scaling is applicable.
  5. If the data value is decimal, then the value as scaled by the binary scaler.  If two scalers were given, the first one is used.
  6. The units in which the data is measured, if any.
  7. A description of what the data is supposed to represent.

As I mentioned, the timestamps provided by analyzeBoost.py on each line aren't as useful as they might be, because they relate to elapsed time in analyzeBoost.py rather than to any timing property of the emulated LVDC.  As it happens, timestamps of a sort are among the telemetry data, but are only emitted periodically rather than being embedded on a PIO-by-PIO basis.  You can actually see one such timestamp in the sample data above: It's D.VTAS, "MISSION TIME AT ACCELEROMETER READ", and its value is close to the timestamp assigned by analyzeBoost.py, but not quite equal. Which should not be a surprise, since given that PIO 000 is emitted only at about 1.5 second intervals, it's not very good for timestamping any individual PIO records appearing in the in the interim.

Besides the timestamps, another inconvenience of analyzeBoost.py is that the telemetry rushes past so fast that you can't possibly see it without scrolling or without capturing it by piping it into file first ... which, on the other hand, is very easy to do.  However, as I said, there is an alternative method of viewing the telemetry, and that's via a GUI program called yaMccLvdc.py, available in the same place in our source-code repository, whose output looks something like the following:



Because each telemetered item stays at the same position on the screen in yaMccLvdc.py, rather than furiously scrolling past you, it's easier to read.  (Though you can no longer capture it to a file as you could have with analyzeBoost.py.)  It's also slightly more analogous to the way mission controllers saw the telemetry back in the day.  On the other hand, because of space limitations, you can no longer have immediate on-screen access to the descriptions of each item, and simply have to know the names of the variables you're interested in.  But as some compensation for that, if you hover the mouse over a variable name, a pop-up window will show its description and other potentially-interesting information, as seen for the variable V.CSB in the screenshot.  The telemetry is alphabetized by variable name, incidentally, so it is relatively easy to find any given variable. Another handy feature is that if you left-click one of the data readouts with your mouse, you can change its color to make it stand out visually and thus make it easier for you to monitor on an ongoing basis; the colors persist even as the readings update, and they cycle through red, green, blue, and black as you repeatedly click.  For example, only the variables  D.VTAS (mission time elapsed), D.VVS (total inertial velocity), and D.VXS/D.VYS/D.VZS (fixed-space coordinates) are relevant to the altitude and velocity graphs we made above for the repeatable flight test mode, so before taking the screenshot you see above, I had already changed their colors to monitor them.  Yet another convenience feature is that if you hold down the Ctrl key while left-clicking a value, then it will highlight that value with a yellow background for about one second every time the value changes.  An example is V.BTC.  Ctrl-clicking it again stops its the highlighting. 

Invoking yaMccLvdc.py is much the same as invoking analyzeBoost.py:
  1. Open up 2 separate command-line windows.
  2. At one of the command lines, run the LVDC CPU emulator, using whatever command switches are appropriate:  "yaLVDC ...".
  3. At the other command line, run "yaMccLvdc.py --version=N", where again, N=1 for AS-206RAM, 2 for AS-512 (default), or 3 for AS-513.
The telemetry display can be quite large relative to your screen real-estate if, for example, you're confined to a laptop computer rather than to the kind of multi-monitor desktop setup I use.  So yaMccLvdc.py has several optional command-line switches you can use to make telemetry display in a limited monitor setup more palatable.  One thing that may happen is that your window manager may decide simply to size the telemetry window to fit your monitor size, rather than to make it big enough to accommodate all of the telemetry.  By default, the yaMccLvdc.py's telemetry window isn't resizable after it has been created, in which case there's no way to see all of the telemetry.  But if you add the --resize command-line switch, it will let you enlarge the window after it has been created.  It's true that it won't fit entirely on the screen at once after it has been enlarged, but at least you can slide it around to see the entire contents.  And then there's the --font=N switch, which can be used to reduce the font size from the default (which is 10).  This will generally make the telemetry window a lot smaller.  Another consideration is that by default, I spell out all of the units (like METERS/SECOND) in full, causing them to occupy quite a bit of screen width.  But if you use the --abbreviate switch, then some of the units can be abbreviated (for example, M/S), reducing the width somewhat.  Though it's less likely with every passing year, I suppose it's also possible that some computer setups still can't process the UTF-8 (unicode) characters that I use for units such as METERS-1 or METERS/SECOND2.  If that's your situation, you can use the --ascii switch to instead display ASCII-only representations like METERS**-1 and METERS/SECOND**2.  It won't reduce the window size, obviously.

If you want to see all the available command-line options, use the command
yaMccLvdc.py --help
We'll encounter yaMccLvdc.py again in the next section.

Using the Digital Command System (DCS)

Note:  This section is under construction, even more so that most of the rest of the material on this page.  In fact, I'm writing it as I learn about it, reverse engineer it, and implement the "modern" form of it, so everything I will say is iffy and very subject to change!

Data isn't merely sent from the LVDC to mission control via telemetry, but can be sent from mission control to the LVDC as well ... though in the latter case the data is normally called a "command", and it's delivered by what's known as the Digital Command System (DCS).  I suppose I should give a disclaimer right up front that when considered as a kind of interface for user input into the LVDC, DCS leaves a lot to be desired.  For one thing, it is capable of doing quite a lot less then you might imagine ... while at the same time, most of what you can do with it is limited to very specific phases of the mission.  Well, it is what it is, and like Popeye the Sailor, that's all that it is.  A corollary of that is that there's a bit of a steep learning curve as well.  Adjust your expectations accordingly.

Regarding the AS-206RAM mission, I know that it had DCS capability because the comments in the Flight Program's source code do refer to it.  But there's no documentation about it, and nothing in the AS-206RAM Flight Program seems to process it directly.  I suspect that this is due to DCS having been processed back in those early days by the Preflight Program (which we don't have) rather than by the Flight Program (which we do).  In other words, the Preflight Program would have received and processed all of the incoming DCS commands, and just passed along to the Flight Program whatever dribble of information it thought was needed.  I suppose.

Whereas, in later missions the DCS was processed by the Flight Program.  So the amount of information about DCS in the AS-512 and AS-513 missions is a bit more substantial if not 100% complete.  Besides the flight-program source code itself, section 10 of the document "Saturn Instrument Unit: LVDC Equation Defining Document (EDD) for the Saturn IB Flight Program" covers some DCS commands in reasonable detail.  The so-called "Technical Information Summary" documents for individual missions, though not providing any technical details about DCS in depth, sometimes though not always list the specific DCS commands available for the missions, as well as the constraints on when those various commands could be accepted.  For example:

  • AS-501 (Apollo 4)
  • AS-204 (Apollo 5)
  • AS-503 (Apollo 8)
  • AS-504 (Apollo 9)
  • AS-505 (Apollo 10)

Admittedly, the value of a list of available DCS commands is minimal without the ability to actually run the still-unavailable LVDC Flight Programs or Preflight Programs capable of accepting those commands, so only AS-512 (Apollo 17) and AS-513 (Skylab 1) are of much interest at the moment.  You'll notice that those missions are not among any of the documented ones.  With that said, the particular commands available to the AS-512 or AS-513 missions are summarized in the following table, to the extent I've been able to discover or guess what they are:

Command (Octal)
 AS-512
 AS-513
05
 INHIBIT MANEUVER
Inhibit coast phase attitude maneuver.
10
 TIME BASE UPDATE
The time-base time is advanced or retarded by a selected amount.
 FINE TIME BASE UPDATE
See also command 40 below.
11
 NAVIGATION UPDATE
Re-initializes the navigation state vector (position and velocity) at a specified time with data provided by mission control.
12
 GENERALIZED SWITCH SELECTOR
The specified switch-selector function is issued at the first opportunity.
13
 SECTOR DUMP
The contents of the specified memory sector are telemetered.
 MEMORY DUMP
The contents of the specified contiguous memory block are telemetered.  Presumably this is a combined equivalent for the separate SECTOR DUMP and TELEMETER SINGLE MEMORY LOCATION commands found in AS-512.
14
 TELEMETER SINGLE MEMORY LOCATION
The content of a single selected memory location is telemetered.
17
 TIME BASE 8 ENABLE
Initiates time base 8.  I believe that TB8 is the post-S-IVB-separation maneuver, including a propellant dump and safeing sequence.
20
 TERMINATE
Stop DCS processing and reset for a new command.
21
EXECUTE ALTERNATE SEQUENCE
In general, initiates one of (potentially) 15 different preprogrammed alternate sequences.  Specifically, in so far as AS-513 is concerned, I think the only choices are sequences 4A and 4B, but I don't know what either of those sequences actually does.
22
 UPDATE MANEUVER
Change the time for starting the coast phase maneuver.
25
 TIME BASE 6D
Initiates time base 6D.  I have found no specific documentation for TB6D, but I believe that it is an S-IVB ignition restart for TLI.
31
 TARGET UPDATE
Replace targeting quantities for second S-IVB burn.
33
 EXECUTE MANEUVER A
Possibly, initiates a maneuver to local horizontal in a retrograde position.
34
 EXECUTE MANEUVER B
35
EXECUTE GENERALIZED MANEUVER
Initiates one of the following:   Inertial-attitude hold maneuver; track-local-reference orbital maneuver; return to nominal timeline (see below); special maneuver A (see AS-512); special maneuver B (see AS-512).
36
RETURN TO NOMINAL TIMELINE
Returns to the pre-programmed orbital attitude timeline in effect prior to DCS-initiated action having overridden it.
40
 INHIBIT MANEUVER
This appears to be the same as command 05 (see above).  The same subroutine is executed for either one.
 COARSE TIME BASE UPDATE
See also command 10 above.
41
 LADDER MAGNITUDE LIMIT
43
 UPDATE MANEUVER
This appears to be the same as command 22 (see above).  The same subroutine is executed for either one.
45
 INHIBIT WATER CONTROL VALVE LOGIC
Inhibit water valve from changing position.
52
 S-IVB/IU LUNAR IMPACT
Presumably, initiates the maneuver that crashes the S-IVB onto the moon.
53
 SWITCH ANTENNA TO OMNI
Both the PCM and CCS antennas are switched.
54
 SWITCH ANTENNA TO LOW GAIN
Both the PCM and CCS antennas are switched.
55
 SWITCH ANTENNA TO HIGH GAIN
Both the PCM and CCS antennas are switched.
60
 TD & E ENABLE
Inhibits T6 (TLI?) so that TD & E (LM Transposition, Docking, and Ejection) can be accomplished in Earth orbit.

As far as what I said above that DCS being more limited than you might expect is concerned, you can see that in the table above.  For example, noticing that there are DCS commands to download chunks of LVDC memory, you might suppose that there would also be DCS commands to upload chunks of memory ... but there aren't.  So DCS cannot be used for purposes such as patching the software — what for the AGC was known as Erasable Memory Programs (EMPs) — nor even for changing the value of a single variable.  Not unless it had been decided in advance to allow certain limited changes to a particular variable and provide a DCS command dedicated to that specific variable.

How does one send a DCS command to the LVDC?  Well, in the case of the "modern" LVDC emulator, you can use the very same yaMccLvdc.py program we used in the preceding section for viewing telemetry.  The program has an extra DCS window that I didn't show you before, enabled by a command-line switch (--dcs).  The DCS window when emulating AS-513 looks something like the following:



You'll notice in the screenshot that just as with the telemetry window, hovering the mouse over many of the items in the window will give you more information about what those items are.

Along the left side is a series of buttons, one for each of the different DCS mode commands defined for the mission.  Some of these commands require you enter some numerical information to be transmitted to the LVDC along with the command.  NAVIGATION UPDATE is an example of that, as it requires you to enter three components of a velocity vector, three components of a position vector, and a time.  Other commands, such as TERMINATE or INHIBIT WATER VALVE LOGIC don't require you to enter any numerical data at all.  At any rate, once you've entered whatever data is required, you simply click the button, and the command+data is sent to the LVDC, where it reacts to it in whatever way the LVDC software dictates.

Some of the items, you'll notice, are grayed out and/or marked as having data "(TBD)".  That's because the documentation for that particular DCS command is absent or so deficient that details about how to process the required data and pack that data into the transmission simply aren't known, and will have to be deduced from the LVDC Flight Program itself ... sometime, but not yet.  It's a work in progress.

Another annoyance from from the standpoint of someone just wanting to emulate, is that most of the DCS commands are not accepted most of the time.  I don't have enough documentation for AS-512 or AS-513 to give you an authoritative list of when commands are accepted and when they are not, but consider the following excerpt from an Apollo 10 (AS-505) table listing the acceptance times:



We see, for example, that the MANEUVER INHIBIT command is accepted from the beginning of time base 5 until 9 seconds before the end of time base 5, and then again from the beginning of time base 7 until the end of mission.  Pretty specific!  Perhaps still worse from our perspective, is that the entirety of our repeatable flight-test simulation is a period of non-acceptance!  It's actually tough to find any time when we can sent a DCS command to the LVDC and expect it to be accepted.

As it happens, we do have means to cheat, and hence to fool the flight program into accepting our DCS command even if it doesn't want to.  At least to a certain extent.  The Flight Program disqualifies DCS commands using several techniques, and we can relatively-painlessly fool one of those techniques.

TBD

Plea for Data

As you will have noted if you've read this far, there are some pretty serious gaps in the publicly-accessible data about the LVDC and its software.  If you know where to find any more information, please tell me about it.  Examples of some of the things that would be interesting to have include:
  • Source code, source code, source code.  Any computer source code—or for that matter, binary code—that ran on the LVDC would be useful:
    • Flight software.
    • Preflight software.
    • Test & checkout software.
    • Even sample code.
  • Manuals explaning the syntax of LVDC assembly-language.
  • Source code for the software run by the test & checkout computers, as opposed to the LVDC itself.
  • Any documents missing from the Document Library.
  • Developers' notes.

Personnel and Development Environment

(This section is under development.)

Let me pause for a moment to summarize, reiterate, and enlarge upon some of the points I've already covered above:
  • The LVDC was a product of IBM Federal Systems Division in Owego, New York.  It nevertheless appears as though many (or perhaps all) of the LVDC software developers resided in Huntsville, Alabama.
  • The software they developed comprised several semi-independent programs, including the LVDC's "Flight Program" (FP), "Pre-Flight Program" (PF), and possibly an "Executive Control Program" (ECP).  By "semi-independent", I mean that while these programs were co-resident in the LVDC memory, they had only limited interactions with each other during usage.  Moreover, they were developed by separate groups, and those development groups had no need to interact with each other as long as the rules for interfacing between the different software programs were rigidly obeyed.
  • At the beginning, the LVDC software for each mission was ad hoc, though adapted from the software for a preceding mission.  Later, however, a "Generalized Flight Program" (GFP) was created, which incorporated the features from preceding missions in a more consistent, configurable way.
As far as the FP is concerned, it seems that development was carried out by two separate teams.  These teams leapfrogged through the missions; i.e., perhaps oversimplifying, one team worked on the odd-numbered missions, while the other teams worked on the even-numbered missions.  At least for the early Apollo missions, these teams generally consisted of two developers, sometimes with a third "newbie" added if the lead developer was preparing to leave the team.  By the time of the Skylab missions, teams usually had four software developers.
